      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jamie Stewart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jamie Stewart" href="/user/show/15954768-jamie-stewart">Jamie Stewart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2755144777&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2755144777" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7437390797710467976">A compact horror novel that continues some great and lingering moments of body horror. Thinner is a story about an overweight lawyer, Billy Halleck. Can we say white privilege. Billy is cursed by a Gypsies curse to lose weight after accidentally running over one of their community. <br/><br/>King taps into the fear of what would happen if a person’s body betrayed them by developing a illness. It’s also one of the few stories that King reflexes his description for gore to great effect. Despite being the Ki</span>
  <span id="freeText7437390797710467976" style="display:none">A compact horror novel that continues some great and lingering moments of body horror. Thinner is a story about an overweight lawyer, Billy Halleck. Can we say white privilege. Billy is cursed by a Gypsies curse to lose weight after accidentally running over one of their community. <br /><br />King taps into the fear of what would happen if a person’s body betrayed them by developing a illness. It’s also one of the few stories that King reflexes his description for gore to great effect. Despite being the King of Horror I find there are only a few of his books that actually feature gruesome scenes of gory and this is one of them. My only issue with it is that for long sections of the novel I don’t find myself engaged. As the plot revolves about Billy discovering what is long with him these long sections are about Billy getting from point A to B in his discovers. They are perked up by the episodes of body horror previously mentioned, but they fail to be compelling for me. An example of this is when Billy finds out what is wrong with him, learns where the Gypsy family are and travels to confront them. It takes him so long to move between these points that I was pulling at my hair. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7437390797710467976" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2441739633" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2441739633" />
    <a title="Kaila" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35924884-kaila"><img alt="Kaila" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1533304308p2/35924884.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2441739633?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kaila" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kaila" href="/user/show/35924884-kaila">Kaila</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35924884-kaila?shelf=paranormal">paranormal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35924884-kaila?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35924884-kaila?shelf=morally-ambiguous">morally-ambiguous</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35924884-kaila?shelf=stephen-king">stephen-king</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35924884-kaila?shelf=physical-extremes">physical-extremes</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35924884-kaila?shelf=shocking-endings">shocking-endings</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35924884-kaila?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2441739633" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12684761019767012196"><b>4/5 stars</b><br/><br/><b>
  <blockquote>“In many ways the world is nothing but a pile of shit. But it can also be very beautiful.”</blockquote>
</b><br/><br/>Oh my god, I just flew through this book. It was just so <b>fast-paced, gripping and entertaining</b>, I really didn't want to put it down. As soon as I read the synopsis of this book I knew that it sounded like something I'd enjoy. It's a weird concept but when you really think about it, it's also terrifying. Basically, our main character has been cursed to lose weight...forever, with no end in sight</span>
  <span id="freeText12684761019767012196" style="display:none"><b>4/5 stars</b><br><br><b>
  <blockquote>“In many ways the world is nothing but a pile of shit. But it can also be very beautiful.”</blockquote>
</b><br><br>Oh my god, I just flew through this book. It was just so <b>fast-paced, gripping and entertaining</b>, I really didn't want to put it down. As soon as I read the synopsis of this book I knew that it sounded like something I'd enjoy. It's a weird concept but when you really think about it, it's also terrifying. Basically, our main character has been cursed to lose weight...forever, with no end in sight. We watch as he wastes away to almost nothing but still maintains the drive and determination to remove the curse. What I really enjoyed about this book is that I didn't know who to root for. All of our main characters are somewhat morally grey and their intentions are not always the greatest, but that just made for a more interesting read. Most of all. It was the ending that has left me <b>completely speechless and shocked to the core.</b><br><br>Billy Halleck is a pretty average American man. He’s a lawyer, has a wife and a daughter but he may also be about 50 pounds overweight, but that's alright with him. One fateful moment changes his life and he can’t help but wish he was back to that 50 pound overweight self of his. When driving one day he accidentally hits a Gypsy woman and kills her. As he is friends with the judge, he got off completely free, but the consequences don’t end there. Unhappy with their treatment in town already and now they injustice of the trial, the Gypsies will not let Billy go free. Instead, the dad of the Gypsy woman curses Billy to get thinner. As he suddenly loses weight uncontrollably and goes from overweight to a skeletal man, Billy must try to find the Gypsy man and find a way to reverse the curse. Either that, or he wastes away completely<br><br><b>
  <blockquote>“But it's hard for a man to give up all his pleasures, even when they don't pleasure him no more.” </blockquote>
</b><br><br>I can never fault King for the premises that he thinks up. This was another very interesting book which made me just fall in love with the plot. I’ve never read anything like this, which immediately made so <b>captivated</b> in the story. This book also slowly gets more high stakes as time goes on and Billy keeps losing weight. As he got more desperate, my reading became more <b>frenzied</b> because I just had to know what was going to happen. I found the plot <b>so darn interesting and compelling</b>, I just had to read it and find out how things would end up. This also made me think about the craze of losing weight and being skinny. This flipped that completely on its head, because Billy went passed ‘ideal weight’ and through to severely unhealthy territory. The only thing I didn’t like about the plot of this book was the focus on peoples weight. As the book focused on Billy losing weight, there were many comments about the weight and appearance of characters which I just found unnecessary and it made me uncomfortable.<br><br>The characters in this book were interesting and they all danced on the line of likeable and unlikeable. Billy was a nice enough character, but he had also done a very bad thing. He was quite racist and self-absorbed at the beginning of the book, but this soon changed and he became much more thoughtful. Then again, he also does questionable things later in the book. The old Gypsy man was at first considered bad because he had cursed Billy and had this kind of creepy aura, but we soon realise he was just trying to protect his family against the prosecution that they had endured their whole lives. Similarly, Heidi (Billy’s wife) just wanted the best for him but she was also pretty horrible. This moral ambiguity of the characters made for a really interesting dynamic which I enjoyed exploring as the book progressed.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1512243960ra/24586769.gif" width="400" height="250" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The most interesting character in this book, at least for me, was Richie Ginnelli. Another very morally ambiguous character, especially since he was from the mob and had no qualms about hurting people. He was such a <b>terrific character</b> who had a charming personality but also a darker side. When he found out what happened to Billy, he tried his hardest to help him, in his own way of course. He was an <b>unforgettable character</b> and I still can’t decide whether he was good or bad or whether he did what he did yo help Billy or for his own enjoyment.<br><br>The ending of this book was completely shocking and memorable. As I read it, I had a hand clasped tightly over my mouth and I was gaping. I just couldn’t believe what I was reading and was <b>shocked to the very core</b>. I still think about the ending from time to time and I doubt that I’m going to forget it anytime soon.<br><br>Also, I almost forgot, hands down the best part of this book is when Stephen King, writing as Richard Bachman gave himself a shout out: <br><br><b>
  <blockquote>“You were starting to sound a little like a Stephen King novel for a while there.”</blockquote>
</b><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="12684761019767012196" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10187996461403744744">Thinner is my first Richard Bachman novel but from what I've heard Thinner is the most like a Stephen King novel. Obviously Richard Bachman is Stephen King but I've heard the Bachman novels have a different feel to them. <br/><br/>I've actually seen the movie Thinner several times and I was shocked by how faithful to the book that movie is. They even kept the same extremely dark ending. <br/><br/>Billy Halleck is a good husband, loving father and the embodiment of The American Dream...Until he runs down a "gypsy" w</span>
  <span id="freeText10187996461403744744" style="display:none">Thinner is my first Richard Bachman novel but from what I've heard Thinner is the most like a Stephen King novel. Obviously Richard Bachman is Stephen King but I've heard the Bachman novels have a different feel to them. <br /><br />I've actually seen the movie Thinner several times and I was shocked by how faithful to the book that movie is. They even kept the same extremely dark ending. <br /><br />Billy Halleck is a good husband, loving father and the embodiment of The American Dream...Until he runs down a "gypsy" woman killing her and is cursed by her father. Overnight Billy an obese man begins to lose weight...A Lot of weight...<br />And no matter how much he eats the weight just keeps falling off. <br /><br />Now I must say that I myself would mind having this curse for a couple weeks...or months..<br /><br />I'm just kidding....?? <br /><br />Thinner wasn't one of Uncle Stevie's better novels. It dragged a bit in the middle and despite being only a little over 300 pages it seemed too long. But the first hundred pages and the last 50 pages were super entertaining. <br /><br />I'm hoping to in 2020 read at least 2 more Bachman books so I can get a better feel of the differences in his writing style. <br /><br />If you love Uncle Stevie then read Thinner but if you're not just watch the movie instead.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5083400583528230958">4/5 stars, Stephen King is never bad tho <br/><br/>When I first started reading this book it really reminded me of the book Elevation also by Stephen King that I read this year. Both have similar premises, someone getting thinner and thinner and not a whole lot they can do to stop it. But this book was definitely a much darker and heavy take on that idea. Basically what I learned from this book is that people are mean and revenge is never truly the answer. <br/><br/>I enjoyed my read of this, I was content when I w</span>
  <span id="freeText5083400583528230958" style="display:none">4/5 stars, Stephen King is never bad tho <br /><br />When I first started reading this book it really reminded me of the book Elevation also by Stephen King that I read this year. Both have similar premises, someone getting thinner and thinner and not a whole lot they can do to stop it. But this book was definitely a much darker and heavy take on that idea. Basically what I learned from this book is that people are mean and revenge is never truly the answer. <br /><br />I enjoyed my read of this, I was content when I was reading the story and I wanted to keep reading. On the other hand there was nothing particularly special about this book, or this story as there’s another Stephen King book like it. It’s not groundbreaking but it was a nice quick read and reminded me that I just appreciate Stephen King when he’s not writing full out horror. <br /><br />But yes, I just learned that people are mean and make mistakes. The characters in this book all had agendas that clashed, but I liked Ginelli even if I probably shouldn’t have, and no one else particularly. The ending had a large point of proving that revenge is never the answer and that you have to admit your mistakes, and take on the consequences. <br /><br />Overall it was fine! It was a quick read and not groundbreaking or anything but a nice fast read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5083400583528230958" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_78615309" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/78615309" />
    <a title="Maciek" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2969647-maciek"><img alt="Maciek" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1280839159p2/2969647.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/78615309?book_show_action=true">Nov 22, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maciek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maciek" href="/user/show/2969647-maciek">Maciek</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780450058837" href="/book/show/1052797.Thinner">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=own-in-paperback">own-in-paperback</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=owned-books">owned-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=saw-movie">saw-movie</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2969647-maciek?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer78615309" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15980053800846641645"><i>'Thinner,' the old Gypsy man with the rotting nose whispers to William Halleck as Halleck and his wife, Heidi, come out of the courthouse. Just that one word, sent on the wafting, cloying sweetness of his breath. 'Thinner.' And before Halleck can jerk away, the old Gypsy reaches out and caresses his cheek with one twisted finger. His lips spread open like a wound, showing a few tombstone stumps poking out of his gums. They are black and green. His tongue squirms between them and then slides out </i></span>
  <span id="freeText15980053800846641645" style="display:none"><i>'Thinner,' the old Gypsy man with the rotting nose whispers to William Halleck as Halleck and his wife, Heidi, come out of the courthouse. Just that one word, sent on the wafting, cloying sweetness of his breath. 'Thinner.' And before Halleck can jerk away, the old Gypsy reaches out and caresses his cheek with one twisted finger. His lips spread open like a wound, showing a few tombstone stumps poking out of his gums. They are black and green. His tongue squirms between them and then slides out to slick his grinning, bitter lips.<br />Thinner.</i><br /><br />Richard Bachman was not a very nice guy, and "Thinner" shows why. It was the last Bachman novel, before the cover was finally blown and it turned out that Richard Bachman was in fact Stephen King. <br />Or as King prefers to say, his dark half. The fingers that typed out the Bachman books might have been King's, but the one who sent words to the fingertips was old Richie. The creep from grocery stalls, airports and train stations, whose novels were printed without any sort of advertising and promotion, and who gained his reputation by word of mouth alone.<br />In fact, "Thinner" sold 28 000 copies when it was published, which is over ten thousand more than King's collection "Night Shift" did several years earlier. Of course, when it turned out that both were copyrighted by the same guy the sales of "Thinner" went through the roof.<br /><br />It's hard for me to choose a favorite from the Bachman books, but I can safely say that Thinner is a perfect closure to a career that ended much too soon. The Bachman novels were all pretty grim, but I think that Thinner, together with The Long Walk, are the most thought-provoking and interesting.<br />Also, Thinner is the sole Bachman novel that can be classified as horror, though it is much more than that.<br /><br />As with "Quitters, inc.", a classic short story about giving up smoking, King took another of the American nightmares and turned it around - weight. Billy Haleck, the protagonist, is a succesful lawyer who also happens to be fifty pounds overweight. Billy is camping at the border of heart attack country; his doctor, his wife, hell everyone tells him to drop some pounds, but Billy just can't get enough of the sweet taste, of the sour taste, of all delicious tastes of all delicious food. After all, all work and no play makes Jack a dull boy!<br /><br />One night while Billy is driving home and his wife, um, distracts him, he hits an old gypsy woman who dies instantly. The case is quickly dismissed; after all, Billy is a lawyer and no one in town is exactly a fan of gypsies. Just as Billy gets out of the courthouse, the old gyp comes to Billy and touches his cheek, whispering the sole, menacing word - "thinner".<br /><br />Billy dismisses the old man as mad, and goes back to his life as if nothing had happened. Till he hits the scale. He notices that he starts losing weight - at first he, his wife and his physician are all happy and rejoice in the blissful success of all diets he undertook. But when the pounds keep dropping and Billy keeps eating he starts being suspicious. He's not afraid of cancer, because his results were perfect...he's afraid that the old gyp might have meant what he said.<br />And the pounds keep dropping...<br /><br />The strenght of "Thinner" lies in its complexity. Most works deal with likable protagonists and unlikable villains. That's not the case with "Thinner" - it's up to the reader to decide whom he will root for. If there is a person to like - After all, Billy did kill the old gyspy woman; and after all, the old gypsy's revenge is much out of proportion. "Thinner" is not a simple horror story, as many would pigeonhole it; the clever meachnisms of characterization and themes allow "Thinner" to work on several levels: as a cautionary tale on the pointlesness of conflict and hatred, the clash of cultures, family drama, corruption...the story of the white man and the gyp who crossed him. Or is it the other way round? <br /><br />I don't want to spoilt any of the plot detail, because it's truly a jou to watch it unfold. The novel was adapted into a movie in 1996, and while it greatly simplifies the novel it carries most of the story thanks to great performances of Robert John Burke and Joe Mantegna-. It's definitely worth seeing, but only after you've read the book. <br />"Thinner" is one of the great horrormeister's most underrated works and deserves to be much better known and appreciated. It will make you turn the page, it will make you think and question your decisions, I don't know if it will scare you but you'll certainly remember it long after turning the last page. <i>Eat hearty.</i><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15980053800846641645" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('H5pZTNgcHoPk9GRJF25onEJqe/FQjBJ39+kBoYvMBS0XSdPX8x6nX2z19NeHv3bX67s/Rx7mb0ZJl7/HostGCQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8PApddGE4RR4LhTbWeL3IPyopQxWVd3okYA3Cv0pZEX4I6Pu+oZYyPAvhEXJM+lrVXnhuhg/oNkv/ols1C4nYQ==')})
  }
//]]>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/5858.Richard_Bachman">About Richard Bachman</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/5858.Richard_Bachman"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1289702449p3/5858.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/5858.Richard_Bachman">Richard Bachman
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
4,284
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer14705539362700643045">This is a <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3389.Stephen_King' title='Stephen King' rel='nofollow noopener'>Stephen King</a> pseudonym.<br/><br/>At the beginning of Stephen King's career, the general view among publishers was that an author was limited to one book per year, since publishing more would be unacceptable to the public. King therefore wanted to write under another name, in order to increase his publication without over-saturating the market for the King "brand". He convinced his publisher, Signe</span>
  <span id="freeText14705539362700643045" style="display:none">This is a <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3389.Stephen_King" title="Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Stephen King</a> pseudonym.<br /><br />At the beginning of Stephen King's career, the general view among publishers was that an author was limited to one book per year, since publishing more would be unacceptable to the public. King therefore wanted to write under another name, in order to increase his publication without over-saturating the market for the King "brand". He convinced his publisher, Signet Books, to print these novels under a pseudonym.<br /><br />In his introduction to <i>The Bachman Books</i>, King states that adopting the nom de plume Bachman was also an attempt to make sense out of his career and try to answer the question of whether his success was due to talent or luck. He says he deliberately released the Bachman novels with as little marketing presence as possible and did his best to "load the dice against" Bachman. King concludes that he has yet to find an answer to the "talent versus luck" question, as he felt he was outed as Bachman too early to know. The Bachman book <i>Thinner</i> (1984) sold 28,000 copies during its initial run—and then ten times as many when it was revealed that Bachman was, in fact, King.<br /><br />The pseudonym King originally selected (Gus Pillsbury) is King's maternal grandfather's name, but at the last moment King changed it to Richard Bachman. Richard is a tribute to crime author Donald E. Westlake's long-running pseudonym Richard Stark. (The surname Stark was later used in King's novel <i>The Dark Half</i>, in which an author's malevolent pseudonym, "George Stark", comes to life.) Bachman was inspired by Bachman–Turner Overdrive, a rock and roll band King was listening to at the time his publisher asked him to choose a pseudonym on the spot.<br /><br />King provided biographical details for Bachman, initially in the "about the author" blurbs in the early novels. Known "facts" about Bachman were that he was born in New York, served a four-year stint in the Coast Guard, which he then followed with ten years in the merchant marine. Bachman finally settled down in rural central New Hampshire, where he ran a medium-sized dairy farm, writing at night. His fifth novel was dedicated to his wife, Claudia Inez Bachman, who also received credit for the bogus author photo on the book jacket. Other "facts" about the author were revealed in publicity dispatches from Bachman's publishers: the Bachmans had one child, a boy, who died in an unfortunate, Stephen King-ish type accident at the age of six, when he fell through a well and drowned. In 1982, a brain tumour was discovered near the base of Bachman's brain; tricky surgery removed it. After Bachman's true identity was revealed, later publicity dispatches (and about the author blurbs) revealed that Bachman died suddenly in late 1985 of "cancer of the pseudonym, a rare form of schizonomia".<br /><br />King dedicated Bachman's early books—<i>Rage (1977), The Long Walk (1979), Roadwork (1981)</i>, and <i>The Running Man</i> (1982)—to people close to him. The link between King and his shadow writer was exposed after a Washington, D.C. bookstore clerk, Steve Brown, noted similarities between the writing styles of King and Bachman. Brown located publisher's records at the Library of Congress which included a document naming King as the author of one of Bachman's novels. Brown wrote to King's publishers with a copy of the documents he had uncovered, and asked them what to do. Two weeks later, King telephoned Brown personally and suggested he write an article about how he discovered the truth, allowing himself to be interviewed. King has taken full ownership of the Bachman name on numerous occasions, as with the republication of the first four Bachman titles as <i> The Bachman Books: Four Early Novels by Stephen King</i> in 1985. The introduction, titled "Why I Was Bachman," details the whole Bachman/King story.<br /><br />Source: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Bachmanhttps://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Bachman">https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="14705539362700643045" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
